For Type_of_Cancer, be SPECIFIC — include as much of the following as the note provides:
1. Histologic subtype: IDC (invasive ductal carcinoma), ILC (invasive lobular carcinoma), DCIS, mixed, etc.
2. Receptor status — ER, PR, and HER2. e.g., "ER+/PR+/HER2-"
3. If receptor status changed between original diagnosis and metastatic/recurrent biopsy, note BOTH.
- Example: "ER+/PR+/HER2- invasive ductal carcinoma" NOT just "breast cancer"
- Example with change: "Originally ER+/PR+/HER2-, metastatic biopsy ER+/PR-/HER2-"
Do NOT write just "breast cancer" or "MBC" — always include the subtype and receptor status when available in the note.
IMPORTANT: Search the ENTIRE note for receptor status — it may appear in PMH, Problem List, Pathology, HPI, or Diagnosis section, not just the Assessment. If ER/PR/HER2 status is stated ANYWHERE, include it. Do NOT omit HER2 status just because it appears only in the Past Medical History.

HER2 status rules — follow strictly:
- If the note explicitly states HER2 result (positive, negative, equivocal, IHC score, FISH result), write it.
- If HER2 was NOT tested or NOT mentioned in the note, write "HER2: not tested" — do NOT guess or assume HER2- or HER2+.
- DCIS (ductal carcinoma in situ) often does NOT have HER2 testing. Do NOT assume HER2- for DCIS unless explicitly stated.
- IMPORTANT: IHC 2+ with FISH NEGATIVE = HER2 negative. Do NOT write HER2+ for IHC 2+/FISH negative.
- IMPORTANT: If treatment includes Herceptin/trastuzumab/pertuzumab/T-DM1/Tykerb/lapatinib/TCHP/THP, the cancer is HER2+. Do NOT write HER2-.
- IMPORTANT: If the note says "triple negative" ANYWHERE (HPI, A/P, diagnosis), that means ER-/PR-/HER2-. Write HER2 as NEGATIVE even if the HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] or masked. "Triple negative" is a definitive classification.
- IMPORTANT: If HER2 IHC/FISH data is [REDACTED] but the Assessment/Plan writes "[REDACTED] negative" in the receptor status line, infer HER2 = negative.

- Write the AJCC/TNM stage if stated (e.g., "Stage IIA", "pT2N0M0", "Stage IV")
- If the patient was originally diagnosed at one stage but now has metastatic recurrence, write BOTH:
  e.g., "Originally Stage IIA, now metastatic (Stage IV)"
- If distant metastases are documented, the current stage is Stage IV even if not explicitly stated.
- If the Assessment/Plan states a stage (e.g., "Stage I", "early stage"), extract it — do NOT write "Not mentioned in note".
- If tumor size and node status are available, infer the approximate stage (e.g., "2.3 cm, 0/3 LN negative — approximately Stage I-II").
- IMPORTANT: Node-negative ("node neg", "0/N LN+") with small tumor (≤2cm) = Stage I, NOT Stage II.
  Stage II requires either positive lymph nodes OR tumor >2cm. "Early stage node negative" = Stage I.
- Only write "Not mentioned in note" if there is truly NO staging, tumor size, or node information anywhere in the note.

Write the INTENT of the cancer treatment itself: "curative", "palliative", "risk reduction", "adjuvant", or "symptom management".
Do NOT write the purpose of this visit (e.g., "follow-up", "discuss treatment options", "review treatment plan").

- If the note says "adjuvant" → this describes the treatment TYPE, not the GOAL. For non-metastatic cancer, adjuvant treatment has curative intent → write "curative". Only write "palliative" if metastatic.
- If the note explicitly states an intent, USE IT. Do NOT override the physician's own words.

ONLY if no explicit intent word is found, use this decision tree:
- Early-stage cancer + surgery + adjuvant chemo/radiation/endocrine therapy → "curative" or "adjuvant"
- Neoadjuvant chemotherapy before surgery for curable cancer → "curative" (NOT "neoadjuvant")
- Metastatic/Stage IV cancer → "palliative"
- DCIS only, no invasive cancer → "risk reduction"
- Completed curative treatment, now on surveillance endocrine therapy → "adjuvant" or "risk reduction"
- Do NOT write "risk reduction" for early-stage invasive cancer receiving adjuvant chemo — that is "curative" or "adjuvant".
- Do NOT write "neoadjuvant" as a goal — write "curative" instead.

', 'Response_Assessment': 'TASK: Extract how the cancer is CURRENTLY responding to treatment.

Think step by step before answering:
1. Has this patient started any cancer treatment yet? (surgery alone does not count as "treatment" for response assessment purposes)
   IMPORTANT: Completed radiation therapy COUNTS as treatment. If the patient recently finished radiation, they HAVE received treatment — do NOT write "Not yet on treatment". "Not yet on treatment" is ONLY correct if no cancer treatment (chemo, radiation, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy) has been given at all.
   IMPORTANT: A patient who was on PREVIOUS treatment and now shows disease PROGRESSION or RECURRENCE — that IS a response assessment (the response is "progression"). Even if they are switching to a new regimen or between treatments, document the current disease status from imaging/labs.
   For LOCAL RECURRENCE: describe the recurrence factually (e.g., "local recurrence in left breast, 1.7cm IDC on excisional biopsy"). Do NOT write "cancer is not responding to treatment" — the patient may have discontinued treatment or completed it years ago. Focus on WHAT happened (recurrence), not WHY.
   IMPORTANT: For patients on adjuvant/maintenance therapy, "no evidence of recurrence" or "no evidence of disease" on exam or imaging IS a valid response assessment — it means the treatment is working.
   IMPORTANT — the patient IS on treatment if ANY of these clues appear:
   - "currently on [drug]", "taking [drug]", "continue [drug]", "on [drug] since [date]"
   - Mentions of chemotherapy cycles, off weeks, dose adjustments (e.g., "off week", "cycle 3", "due to start next cycle")
   - "labs okay to proceed with chemotherapy" or similar pre-cycle clearance
   - Current medications include any oncologic drug (chemo, hormonal, targeted, immunotherapy)
   - Active medication list contains chemotherapy drugs (Xeloda, ixabepilone, paclitaxel, etc.)
   Do NOT be confused by "off week" — a patient on an off week between chemo cycles IS on treatment.
2. If yes, what treatment are they on? (chemo, endocrine therapy, targeted therapy, immunotherapy)
3. Is there any evidence of how the cancer is responding? Search the ENTIRE note for ANY of these:
   - Imaging: CT stable, PET response, scan results
   - Tumor markers: CA 15-3, CEA rising or falling
   - Physical exam: mass shrinking, "breaking up", palpable changes, skin changes improving
   - Clinical status: "labs okay to proceed" (implies stable enough to continue)
   - Symptom changes: "able to do more", "pain improving", "up and about"
   Even brief exam comments like "area appears to be breaking up" count as response evidence.
4. If no treatment started yet, write "Not yet on treatment — no response to assess."
5. If on treatment but no response data in the note, write "Not mentioned in note."

STRICT RULES — do NOT violate:
- Do NOT include future plans ("will start", "plan to", "she will have radiation").
- Do NOT include treatment discussions or recommendations.
- Do NOT use generic boilerplate phrases. Every claim must be traceable to a specific sentence in the note.
- Do NOT write "tolerating therapy well" or "symptoms improved" unless the note EXPLICITLY uses those exact words.
- Do NOT describe pathology results (tumor size, grade, margins) as a "response" — pathology is a diagnosis, not a treatment response.
- Do NOT describe surgical recovery ("healing well", "recovering from surgery") as a response assessment.
- Do NOT describe risk estimates or Oncotype scores as a response — those are prognostic tools, not treatment responses.
- Do NOT write "Not applicable" or "Not specified".
Examples:
- GOOD: "CT stable, CA 15-3 decreased from 28 to 14"
- GOOD: "no evidence of disease recurrence on imaging and exam"
- GOOD: "PET-CT shows stable to slightly decreased metabolic activity of osseous metastases"
- GOOD: "Axillary LN decreased from 8cm to 2-3cm after 2 cycles AC"
- BAD: "tolerating therapy well, symptoms improved" (generic boilerplate)
- BAD: "She will continue on letrozole and have radiation" (this is a plan, not a response)
- BAD: "0.9 cm grade 1 IDC, margins negative" (this is pathology, not treatment response)
- BAD: "Healing well from surgery" (surgical recovery, not cancer treatment response)
- BAD: "Low Risk Oncotype score" (prognostic tool, not a response to treatment)

Write your reasoning first, then output the JSON on a new line starting with {
IMPORTANT: Your JSON must contain ONLY the key "response_assessment". Do NOT add any other keys.

Respond with a JSON object using this exact schema (NO other keys):
{
"response_assessment": "How is the cancer CURRENTLY responding? Cite specific evidence: imaging, labs, exam. Do NOT include future plans, surgical recovery, or prognostic scores."
}

Search for ALL of these keywords: "refer", "referral", "consult", "counseling", "social work", "exercise", "dental", "physical therapy", "PT", "OT", "psychiatry", "psychology", "palliative".
Common referrals found OUTSIDE the A/P section:
- "Ambulatory Referral to Social Work" (in Diagnosis section) → Others: Social work referral
- "Referral to Exercise Counseling" (in Diagnosis section) → Others: Exercise counseling referral
- "I will refer to social work" (in Psychologic section) → Others: Social work referral

CRITICAL — distinguish OUTGOING from INCOMING referrals:
- OUTGOING (include): This note's doctor is SENDING the patient elsewhere. E.g., "I referred her to Rad Onc", "consult with genetics recommended", "will schedule Rad Onc consult".
- INCOMING (do NOT include): The patient was SENT TO this doctor by someone else. E.g., "referred by Dr. Smith", "referred to us for evaluation", "referred for consideration of adjunct therapy".
- Do NOT include the current visit's own department. These notes are written by Medical Oncology — do NOT list "Medical Oncology" or "Med Onc" as a referral.
- If the note says "referred to Med Onc (today's visit) and also Rad Onc", only include "Rad Onc" — the Med Onc part describes THIS visit, not an outgoing referral.

If no referral is mentioned for a category, set its value to "None".
For Nutrition: only include referrals to a nutritionist or dietitian (e.g., "nutrition consult", "refer to nutritionist", "follow up with nutrition on [date]").
The following are NOT nutrition referrals — they are general lifestyle advice from the oncologist:
- "I recommend an anti inflammatory/mediterranean diet" → NOT a referral
- "I recommend a diabetes risk reducing diet" → NOT a referral
- "eat calcium-rich food", "limit alcohol", "exercise" → NOT a referral
- "take calcium and vitamin D", "continue vitamin D daily" → NOT a referral (supplement advice)
- "discussed lifestyle modifications" → NOT a referral
If the oncologist gives diet advice without referring to a nutritionist/dietitian, write "None".
Respond *only* with a JSON object using this exact schema:
{
"Nutrition": "any nutrition referrals such as diet optimization, appetite, weight maintenance, or 'None'",
"Genetics": "ONLY outgoing referrals TO a genetics clinic or counselor (e.g., 'refer to genetics', 'genetics consult recommended'). Do NOT list genetic test RESULTS or known mutations here (e.g., 'BRCA1 mutation', 'ATM mutation carrier', 'genetic testing negative' are FINDINGS, not referrals). Write 'None' if no genetics referral is planned.",
"Specialty": "eg, Palliative care, Radiation oncology consult, surgical oncology, Integrative Medicine, Psychology/psychiatry, Dental evaluation, or 'None'",
"Others": "Physical or occupational therapy, Exercise counseling referral, Social work, financial counseling, home health, or 'None'. IMPORTANT: General advice about exercise ('continue exercise', 'weight bearing exercise', 'discussed exercise') is NOT an exercise referral. Only include if there is an explicit referral to an exercise counseling program (e.g., 'Referral to Exercise Counseling').",
"follow up": "follow up mentioned in the provided text. f/up, f/p and fup all mean follow up."
}
', 'follow_up_next_visit': 'TASK: Extract the 'follow up/next visit' from the given 'Assessment/Plan' section.

    "Next clinic visit": "(in-person or telehealth): timing and purpose"

IMPORTANT: Code status is often found OUTSIDE the Assessment/Plan section — look in the FULL NOTE for:
- "Code status: Full code" or "Full code"
- "DNR" or "DNI" or "DNR/DNI"

Side effects are often predictable in terms of their onset and duration.  Side effects are almost always reversible and will go away after treatment is complete. Sensory loss, numbness and tingling, and difficulty in walking may last for at least as long as therapy is continued. These side effects may become progressively more severe with continued treatment, and your doctor may decide to decrease your dose.  Central neurotoxicity:  Infrequent but patients over age 65 are at increased risk.  Symptoms include dizziness, confusion, visual changes, ringing in the ears. Although infrequent, heart failure, blood clots and strokes have been reported with Carboplatin use.  Less than 1% were life-threatening.   Allergic reaction may occur. Outside the context of a clinical trial, we would recommend AC/T. We typically give the taxol first, followed by four cycles of TC. We then explained the option of clinical trial enrollment with the ISPY clinical trial. We explained that some of these arms involve getting taxol with an experimental therapy. Patients will also undergo additional MRIs during the course of neoadjuvant chemotherapy to assist in assessing response to treatment. We discussed the very ***** trial design and explained that if the patient is randomized to the non-paclitaxel \n containing arm (such as ***** or *****), they can switch back to paclitaxel if not responding or receive it post operatively. In any of the arms, if the patient appears to have progression of her tumor either by physical exam or breast imaging, there is the option of discontinuing the study treatment and either moving on to the AC or switching to paclitaxel.     We reviewed the anticipated side effects and toxicities of chemotherapy including increased risk of infection, hair loss, fatigue, and possibility of ovarian failure. We explained that anthracyclines are associated with a small chance of causing permanent damage to the heart (estimated to be about 1% with 4 cycles of adriamycin and cyclophosphamide) and that ***** are associated with development of neuropathy which is most often transient and rarely permanent.    We also mentioned that given she was ER/PR low positive, we would still recommend 5-10 years of endocrine therapy after surgery. We did not discuss the options in length today, but can do so at a later date.    She asked about whether she could continue to work as an ICU nurse during chemo. We advised her that many patients choose to work during weekly taxol, but it will largely depend on which arm she is randomized to, as some of the experimental arms are likely to be associated with more toxicity. Most patients choose to take off time during AC and then after recovering from chemotherapy.
